      <p><b>South Africa</b>, officially the <b>Republic of South Africa</b> (<b>RSA</b>), is the southernmost country in <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>. With over <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_South_Africa" title="Demographics of South Africa">59&nbsp;million people</a>, it is the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_population" class="mw-redirect" title="List of countries by population">23rd-most populous nation</a> and covers an area of 1,221,037 square kilometres (471,445 square miles). South Africa has three capital cities: executive <a href="/wiki/Pretoria" title="Pretoria">Pretoria</a>, judicial <a href="/wiki/Bloemfontein" title="Bloemfontein">Bloemfontein</a> and legislative <a href="/wiki/Cape_Town" title="Cape Town">Cape Town</a>. The largest city is <a href="/wiki/Johannesburg" title="Johannesburg">Johannesburg</a>. About 80% of South Africans are of <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples_in_South_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Bantu peoples in South Africa">Black African</a> ancestry,<sup id="cite_ref-cib11_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cib11-9">[9]</a></sup> divided among a <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_South_Africa" title="Ethnic groups in South Africa">variety of ethnic groups</a> speaking <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_South_Africa" title="Languages of South Africa">different African languages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-safacts_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-safacts-15">[15]</a></sup> The remaining population consists of Africa's largest communities of <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Europe" title="Demographics of Europe">European</a> (<a href="/wiki/White_South_Africans" title="White South Africans">White South Africans</a>), <a href="/wiki/Asian_people" title="Asian people">Asian</a> (<a href="/wiki/Indian_South_Africans" title="Indian South Africans">Indian South Africans</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Multiracial" class="mw-redirect" title="Multiracial">Multiracial</a> (<a href="/wiki/Coloureds" title="Coloureds">Coloured South Africans</a>) ancestry. </p>
      <p>It is bounded to the south by 2,798 kilometres (1,739&nbsp;mi) of coastline of Southern Africa stretching along the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">South Atlantic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Oceans</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-safacts_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-safacts-15">[15]</a></sup> to the north by the neighbouring countries of <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Botswana" title="Botswana">Botswana</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>; and to the east and northeast by <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eswatini" title="Eswatini">Eswatini</a> (former Swaziland); and it surrounds the <a href="/wiki/Enclave_and_exclave" title="Enclave and exclave">enclaved country</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lesotho" title="Lesotho">Lesotho</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> It is the southernmost country on the mainland of the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a>, and the most populous country located entirely south of the equator. South Africa is a biodiversity hotspot, with a diversity of unique biomes and plant and animal life. </p>
      <p>South Africa is a <a href="/wiki/Multiethnic_society" class="mw-redirect" title="Multiethnic society">multiethnic society</a> encompassing a wide <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_South_Africa" title="Culture of South Africa">variety of cultures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_South_Africa" title="Languages of South Africa">languages</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_South_Africa" title="Religion in South Africa">religions</a>. Its pluralistic makeup is reflected in the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_South_Africa" title="Constitution of South Africa">constitution</a>'s recognition of 11 official languages, the fourth-highest number in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-safacts_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-safacts-15">[15]</a></sup> According to the 2011 census, the two most spoken first languages are <a href="/wiki/Zulu_language" title="Zulu language">Zulu</a> (22.7%) and <a href="/wiki/Xhosa_language" title="Xhosa language">Xhosa</a> (16.0%).<sup id="cite_ref-cib11_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cib11-9">[9]</a></sup> The two next ones are of European origin: <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans" title="Afrikaans">Afrikaans</a> (13.5%) developed from <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> and serves as the first language of most <a href="/wiki/Coloured" class="mw-redirect" title="Coloured">Coloured</a> and <a href="/wiki/White_South_African" class="mw-redirect" title="White South African">White</a> South Africans; <a href="/wiki/South_African_English" title="South African English">English</a> (9.6%) reflects the legacy of <a href="/wiki/British_colonialism" class="mw-redirect" title="British colonialism">British colonialism</a>, and is commonly used in public and commercial life. The country is one of the few in Africa never to have had a coup d'état, and <a href="/wiki/Elections_in_South_Africa" title="Elections in South Africa">regular elections</a> have been held for almost a century. However, the vast majority of black South Africans were not <a href="/wiki/Enfranchised" class="mw-redirect" title="Enfranchised">enfranchised</a> until 1994. </p>
      <p>During the 20th century, the black majority sought to claim more rights from the dominant white minority, which played a large role in the country's recent <a href="/wiki/History_of_South_Africa" title="History of South Africa">history</a> and politics. The <a href="/wiki/National_Party_(South_Africa)" title="National Party (South Africa)">National Party</a> imposed <a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">apartheid</a> in 1948, institutionalising previous racial segregation. After a long and sometimes violent <a href="/wiki/Internal_resistance_to_apartheid" title="Internal resistance to apartheid">struggle</a> by the <a href="/wiki/African_National_Congress" title="African National Congress">African National Congress</a> (ANC) and other anti-apartheid activists both inside and outside the country, the repeal of discriminatory laws began in the mid-1980s. Since 1994, all ethnic and linguistic groups have held political representation in the country's <a href="/wiki/Liberal_democracy" title="Liberal democracy">liberal democracy</a>, which comprises a <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_republic" title="Parliamentary republic">parliamentary republic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_South_Africa" title="Provinces of South Africa">nine provinces</a>. South Africa is often referred to as the "<a href="/wiki/Rainbow_nation" title="Rainbow nation">rainbow nation</a>" to describe the country's multicultural diversity, especially in the wake of apartheid.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>South Africa is a <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing country</a>. It has been classified by the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Newly_industrialised_country" class="mw-redirect" title="Newly industrialised country">newly industrialised country</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/List_of_African_countries_by_GDP_(nominal)" title="List of African countries by GDP (nominal)">third-largest economy</a> in Africa, and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(nominal)" title="List of countries by GDP (nominal)">33rd-largest in the world</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Waugh2000_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Waugh2000-21">[21]</a></sup> South Africa also has the most <a href="/wiki/List_of_World_Heritage_Sites_in_Africa" title="List of World Heritage Sites in Africa">UNESCO World Heritage Sites</a> in Africa. The country is a <a href="/wiki/Middle_power" title="Middle power">middle power</a> in international affairs; it maintains significant <a href="/wiki/Regional_power" title="Regional power">regional influence</a> and is a member of both the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a> and <a href="/wiki/G20" title="G20">G20</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lynch2010_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lynch2010-23">[23]</a></sup> However, crime, poverty and <a href="/wiki/Inequality_in_post-apartheid_South_Africa" title="Inequality in post-apartheid South Africa">inequality</a> remain widespread, with about a quarter of the population unemployed and living on less than US$1.25 a day.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Moreover, <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_South_Africa" title="Climate change in South Africa">climate change is an important issue for South Africa</a>: it is a major contributor to climate change as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_greenhouse_gas_emissions" title="List of countries by greenhouse gas emissions">14th largest emitter of greenhouse gases</a> as of 2018 (in large part due to its <a href="/wiki/Coal_in_South_Africa" title="Coal in South Africa">coal industry</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> and is vulnerable to many of its impacts, because of its water-insecure environment and vulnerable communities. </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Official_names_of_South_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Official names of South Africa">Official names of South Africa</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The name "South Africa" is derived from the country's geographic location at the southern tip of Africa. Upon formation, the country was named the <a href="/wiki/Union_of_South_Africa" title="Union of South Africa">Union of South Africa</a> in English and <i lang="nl" title="Dutch-language text">Unie van Zuid-Afrika</i> in <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>, reflecting its origin from the unification of four formerly separate British colonies. Since 1961, the long formal name in English has been the "Republic of South Africa" and <i lang="af" title="Afrikaans-language text">Republiek van Suid-Afrika</i> in <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans" title="Afrikaans">Afrikaans</a>. Since 1994, the country has had an official name in each of its <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_South_Africa" title="Languages of South Africa">11 official languages</a>. </p>
      <p><b>Mzansi</b>, derived from the <a href="/wiki/Xhosa_language" title="Xhosa language">Xhosa</a> noun <i lang="xh" title="Xhosa-language text">uMzantsi</i> meaning "south", is a <a href="/wiki/Colloquialism" title="Colloquialism">colloquial name</a> for South Africa,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> while some <a href="/wiki/Pan-Africanism" title="Pan-Africanism">Pan-Africanist</a> political parties prefer the term "<a href="/wiki/Azania" title="Azania">Azania</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_South_Africa" title="History of South Africa">History of South Africa</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistoric_archaeology">Prehistoric archaeology</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:South_Africa_-_population_migrations.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/South_Africa_-_population_migrations.svg/220px-South_Africa_-_population_migrations.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/South_Africa_-_population_migrations.svg/330px-South_Africa_-_population_migrations.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/South_Africa_-_population_migrations.svg/440px-South_Africa_-_population_migrations.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="460" data-file-height="440"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:South_Africa_-_population_migrations.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Migrations that formed the modern <a href="/wiki/Rainbow_nation" title="Rainbow nation">Rainbow nation</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>South Africa contains some of the oldest archaeological and <a href="/wiki/Human_fossil" class="mw-redirect" title="Human fossil">human-fossil</a> sites in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Archaeologists have recovered extensive <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> remains from a series of caves in <a href="/wiki/Gauteng_Province" class="mw-redirect" title="Gauteng Province">Gauteng Province</a>. The area, a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_site" class="mw-redirect" title="UNESCO World Heritage site">UNESCO World Heritage site</a>, has been branded "the <a href="/wiki/Cradle_of_Humankind" title="Cradle of Humankind">Cradle of Humankind</a>". The sites include <a href="/wiki/Sterkfontein" title="Sterkfontein">Sterkfontein</a>, one of the richest sites for <a href="/wiki/Hominin" class="mw-redirect" title="Hominin">hominin</a> fossils in the world. Other sites include <a href="/wiki/Swartkrans" title="Swartkrans">Swartkrans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gondolin_Cave" title="Gondolin Cave">Gondolin Cave</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kromdraai" class="mw-redirect" title="Kromdraai">Kromdraai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coopers_Cave" class="mw-redirect" title="Coopers Cave">Coopers Cave</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malapa" class="mw-redirect" title="Malapa">Malapa</a>. <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Dart" title="Raymond Dart">Raymond Dart</a> identified the first hominin fossil discovered in Africa, the <a href="/wiki/Taung_Child" title="Taung Child">Taung Child</a> (found near <a href="/wiki/Taung" title="Taung">Taung</a>) in 1924. Further hominin remains have come from the sites of <a href="/wiki/Makapansgat" title="Makapansgat">Makapansgat</a> in <a href="/wiki/Limpopo" title="Limpopo">Limpopo</a> Province, <a href="/wiki/Cornelia,_Free_State" title="Cornelia, Free State">Cornelia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Florisbad" title="Florisbad">Florisbad</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Free_State_(South_African_province)" class="mw-redirect" title="Free State (South African province)">Free State Province</a>, <a href="/wiki/Border_Cave" title="Border Cave">Border Cave</a> in <a href="/wiki/KwaZulu-Natal_Province" class="mw-redirect" title="KwaZulu-Natal Province">KwaZulu-Natal Province</a>, <a href="/wiki/Klasies_River_Mouth" class="mw-redirect" title="Klasies River Mouth">Klasies River Mouth</a> in Eastern Cape Province and <a href="/wiki/Pinnacle_Point" title="Pinnacle Point">Pinnacle Point</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elandsfontein_cranium" class="mw-redirect" title="Elandsfontein cranium">Elandsfontein</a> and Die Kelders Cave in <a href="/wiki/Western_Cape_Province" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Cape Province">Western Cape Province</a>. </p>
      <p>These finds suggest that various hominid species existed in South Africa from about three million years ago, starting with <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_africanus" title="Australopithecus africanus">Australopithecus africanus</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> There followed species including <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_sediba" title="Australopithecus sediba">Australopithecus sediba</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_ergaster" title="Homo ergaster">Homo ergaster</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_rhodesiensis" title="Homo rhodesiensis">Homo rhodesiensis</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_helmei" class="mw-redirect" title="Homo helmei">Homo helmei</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_naledi" title="Homo naledi">Homo naledi</a></i> and modern humans (<i><a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" class="mw-redirect" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i>). <a href="/wiki/Modern_humans" class="mw-redirect" title="Modern humans">Modern humans</a> have <a href="/wiki/Pinnacle_Point" title="Pinnacle Point">inhabited Southern Africa</a> for at least 170,000 years. </p>
      <p>Various researchers have located <a href="/wiki/Pebble_tool" class="mw-redirect" title="Pebble tool">pebble tools</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Vaal_River" title="Vaal River">Vaal River</a> valley.<sup id="cite_ref-Langer_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Langer-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bantu_expansion">Bantu expansion</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:MapungubweHill.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/MapungubweHill.jpg/220px-MapungubweHill.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/MapungubweHill.jpg/330px-MapungubweHill.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/MapungubweHill.jpg/440px-MapungubweHill.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="532"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:MapungubweHill.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Mapungubwe_Hill" class="mw-redirect" title="Mapungubwe Hill">Mapungubwe Hill</a>, the site of the former capital of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Mapungubwe" title="Kingdom of Mapungubwe">Kingdom of Mapungubwe</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Settlements of <a href="/wiki/Bantu-speaking_peoples_of_South_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Bantu-speaking peoples of South Africa">Bantu-speaking peoples</a>, who were iron-using <a href="/wiki/Agriculturists" class="mw-redirect" title="Agriculturists">agriculturists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Herder" title="Herder">herdsmen</a>, were already present south of the <a href="/wiki/Limpopo_River" title="Limpopo River">Limpopo River</a> (now the northern border with <a href="/wiki/Botswana" title="Botswana">Botswana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>) by the 4th or 5th century <a href="/wiki/Current_Era" class="mw-redirect" title="Current Era">CE</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Bantu_expansion" title="Bantu expansion">Bantu expansion</a>). They displaced, conquered, and absorbed the original <a href="/wiki/Khoisan" title="Khoisan">Khoisan</a> speakers, the <a href="/wiki/Khoikhoi" class="mw-redirect" title="Khoikhoi">Khoikhoi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bushmen" class="mw-redirect" title="Bushmen">San</a> peoples. The Bantu slowly moved south. The earliest <a href="/wiki/Ironworks" title="Ironworks">ironworks</a> in modern-day KwaZulu-Natal Province are believed to date from around 1050. The southernmost group was the <a href="/wiki/Xhosa_people" title="Xhosa people">Xhosa people</a>, whose language incorporates certain linguistic traits from the earlier Khoisan people. The Xhosa reached the <a href="/wiki/Great_Fish_River" title="Great Fish River">Great Fish River</a>, in today's Eastern Cape Province. As they migrated, these larger <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> populations displaced or assimilated earlier peoples. In <a href="/wiki/Mpumalanga" title="Mpumalanga">Mpumalanga</a> Province, several stone circles have been found along with the stone arrangement that has been named <a href="/wiki/Adam%27s_Calendar" class="mw-redirect" title="Adam's Calendar">Adam's Calendar</a>, and the ruins are thought to be created by the <a href="/wiki/Bokoni" title="Bokoni">Bakone</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Northern_Sotho_language" title="Northern Sotho language">Northern Sotho</a> people.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Portuguese_exploration">Portuguese exploration</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_discoveries" title="Portuguese discoveries">Portuguese discoveries</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:F._Benda-The_planting_of_cross_by_Bartholomew_Dias_in_1488-0681_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/F._Benda-The_planting_of_cross_by_Bartholomew_Dias_in_1488-0681_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-F._Benda-The_planting_of_cross_by_Bartholomew_Dias_in_1488-0681_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/F._Benda-The_planting_of_cross_by_Bartholomew_Dias_in_1488-0681_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-F._Benda-The_planting_of_cross_by_Bartholomew_Dias_in_1488-0681_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/F._Benda-The_planting_of_cross_by_Bartholomew_Dias_in_1488-0681_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-F._Benda-The_planting_of_cross_by_Bartholomew_Dias_in_1488-0681_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2705" data-file-height="1793"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:F._Benda-The_planting_of_cross_by_Bartholomew_Dias_in_1488-0681_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Portuguese explorer <a href="/wiki/Bartolomeu_Dias" title="Bartolomeu Dias">Bartolomeu Dias</a> planting the cross at <a href="/wiki/Cape_Point" title="Cape Point">Cape Point</a> after being the first to successfully round the <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>At the time of European contact, the dominant ethnic group was Bantu-speaking peoples who migrated from other parts of Africa about one thousand years before. The two major historic groups were the <a href="/wiki/Xhosa_people" title="Xhosa people">Xhosa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zulu_people" title="Zulu people">Zulu peoples</a>. </p>
      <p>In 1487, the Portuguese explorer <a href="/wiki/Bartolomeu_Dias" title="Bartolomeu Dias">Bartolomeu Dias</a> led the first European voyage to land in southern Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-domville-25_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-domville-25-38">[38]</a></sup> On 4 December, he landed at <a href="/wiki/Walfisch_Bay" class="mw-redirect" title="Walfisch Bay">Walfisch Bay</a> (now known as <a href="/wiki/Walvis_Bay" title="Walvis Bay">Walvis Bay</a> in present-day Namibia). This was south of the furthest point reached in 1485 by his predecessor, the Portuguese navigator <i lang="pt" title="Portuguese-language text"><a href="/wiki/Diogo_C%C3%A3o" title="Diogo Cão">Diogo Cão</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Cape_Cross" title="Cape Cross">Cape Cross</a>, north of the bay). Dias continued down the western coast of southern Africa. After 8 January 1488, prevented by storms from proceeding along the coast, he sailed out of sight of land and passed the southernmost point of Africa without seeing it. He reached as far up the eastern coast of Africa as, what he called, <i lang="pt" title="Portuguese-language text">Rio do Infante</i>, probably the present-day <a href="/wiki/Groot_River_(Eastern_Cape)" title="Groot River (Eastern Cape)">Groot River</a>, in May 1488, but on his return he saw the Cape, which he first named <i lang="pt" title="Portuguese-language text">Cabo das Tormentas</i> ('Cape of Storms'). His King, <a href="/wiki/John_II_of_Portugal" title="John II of Portugal">John II</a>, renamed the point <i lang="pt" title="Portuguese-language text">Cabo da Boa Esperança</i>, or <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a>, as it led to the riches of the <a href="/wiki/East_Indies" title="East Indies">East Indies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Dias' feat of navigation was later immortalised in <a href="/wiki/Lu%C3%ADs_de_Cam%C3%B5es" title="Luís de Camões">Luís de Camões</a>' Portuguese epic poem, <i><a href="/wiki/Os_Lus%C3%ADadas" title="Os Lusíadas">The Lusiads</a></i> (1572). </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dutch_colonisation">Dutch colonisation</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Cape_Colony" title="Dutch Cape Colony">Dutch Cape Colony</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boer_Republics" title="Boer Republics">Boer Republics</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Bell_-_Jan_van_Riebeeck_se_aankoms_aan_die_Kaap.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Charles_Bell_-_Jan_van_Riebeeck_se_aankoms_aan_die_Kaap.jpg/220px-Charles_Bell_-_Jan_van_Riebeeck_se_aankoms_aan_die_Kaap.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Charles_Bell_-_Jan_van_Riebeeck_se_aankoms_aan_die_Kaap.jpg/330px-Charles_Bell_-_Jan_van_Riebeeck_se_aankoms_aan_die_Kaap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Charles_Bell_-_Jan_van_Riebeeck_se_aankoms_aan_die_Kaap.jpg/440px-Charles_Bell_-_Jan_van_Riebeeck_se_aankoms_aan_die_Kaap.jpg 2x" data-file-width="989" data-file-height="748"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Bell_-_Jan_van_Riebeeck_se_aankoms_aan_die_Kaap.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Charles_Davidson_Bell" title="Charles Davidson Bell">Charles Davidson Bell</a>'s 19th-century painting of <a href="/wiki/Jan_van_Riebeeck" title="Jan van Riebeeck">Jan van Riebeeck</a>, who founded the first European settlement in South Africa, arrives in <a href="/wiki/Table_Bay" title="Table Bay">Table Bay</a> in 1652
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>By the early 17th century, Portugal's maritime power was starting to decline, and English and Dutch merchants competed to oust Lisbon from its lucrative monopoly on the spice trade.<sup id="cite_ref-Pakeman_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pakeman-40">[40]</a></sup> Representatives of the <a href="/wiki/British_East_India_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="British East India Company">British East India Company</a> did call sporadically at the Cape in search of provisions as early as 1601, but later came to favour <a href="/wiki/Ascension_Island" title="Ascension Island">Ascension Island</a> and <a href="/wiki/St._Helena" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Helena">St. Helena</a> as alternative ports of refuge.<sup id="cite_ref-Kaap_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kaap-41">[41]</a></sup> Dutch interest was aroused after 1647, when two employees of the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_India_Company" title="Dutch East India Company">Dutch East India Company</a> (<a href="/wiki/Verenigde_Oostindische_Compagnie" class="mw-redirect" title="Verenigde Oostindische Compagnie">VOC</a>) were shipwrecked at the Cape for several months. The sailors were able to survive by obtaining fresh water and meat from the natives.<sup id="cite_ref-Kaap_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kaap-41">[41]</a></sup> They also sowed vegetables in the fertile soil.<sup id="cite_ref-zastudy_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zastudy-42">[42]</a></sup> Upon their return to Holland, they reported favourably on the Cape's potential as a "warehouse and garden" for provisions to stock passing ships for long voyages.<sup id="cite_ref-Kaap_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kaap-41">[41]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1652, a century and a half after the discovery of the Cape sea route, <a href="/wiki/Jan_van_Riebeeck" title="Jan van Riebeeck">Jan van Riebeeck</a> established a <i lang="en"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/victual#English" class="extiw" title="wikt:victual">victualling</a></i> station at the <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a>, at what would become <a href="/wiki/Cape_Town" title="Cape Town">Cape Town</a>, on behalf of the Dutch East India Company.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hunt1_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hunt1-44">[44]</a></sup> In time, the Cape became home to a large population of <i lang="nl" title="Dutch-language text">vrijlieden</i>, also known as <i lang="nl" title="Dutch-language text">vrijburgers</i> (<abbr title="literal translation">lit.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap"> </span>'free citizens'), former company employees who stayed in Dutch territories overseas after serving their contracts.<sup id="cite_ref-Hunt1_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hunt1-44">[44]</a></sup> Dutch traders also brought thousands of <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">enslaved people</a> to the fledgling colony from <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, and parts of eastern Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-Worden_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Worden-45">[45]</a></sup> Some of the earliest mixed race communities in the country were formed between <i lang="nl" title="Dutch-language text">vrijburgers</i>, enslaved people, and indigenous peoples.<sup id="cite_ref-zimstudy_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zimstudy-46">[46]</a></sup> This led to the development of a new ethnic group, the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Coloured" class="mw-redirect" title="Cape Coloured">Cape Coloureds</a>, most of whom adopted the Dutch language and Christian faith.<sup id="cite_ref-zimstudy_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zimstudy-46">[46]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The eastward expansion of Dutch colonists ushered in a series of wars with the southwesterly migrating Xhosa tribe, known as the <a href="/wiki/Xhosa_Wars" title="Xhosa Wars">Xhosa Wars</a>, as both sides competed for the pastureland near the <a href="/wiki/Great_Fish_River" title="Great Fish River">Great Fish River</a>, which the colonists required to graze their cattle.<sup id="cite_ref-Stapleton2_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stapleton2-47">[47]</a></sup> <i>Vrijburgers</i> who became independent farmers on the frontier were known as <i><a href="/wiki/Boers" title="Boers">Boers</a></i>, with some adopting semi-nomadic lifestyles being denoted as <i lang="nl" title="Dutch-language text"><a href="/wiki/Trekboers" title="Trekboers">trekboers</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Stapleton2_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stapleton2-47">[47]</a></sup> The Boers formed loose <a href="/wiki/Militia" title="Militia">militias</a>, which they termed <i>commandos</i>, and forged alliances with Khoisan peoples to repel Xhosa raids.<sup id="cite_ref-Stapleton2_47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stapleton2-47">[47]</a></sup> Both sides launched bloody but inconclusive offensives, and sporadic violence, often accompanied by livestock theft, remained common for several decades.<sup id="cite_ref-Stapleton2_47-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stapleton2-47">[47]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_colonisation_and_the_Great_Trek">British colonisation and the Great Trek</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_the_Cape_Colony" title="Invasion of the Cape Colony">Invasion of the Cape Colony</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cape_Colony" title="Cape Colony">Cape Colony</a>, <a href="/wiki/Great_Trek" title="Great Trek">Great Trek</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Bechuanaland" title="British Bechuanaland">British Bechuanaland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Colony_of_Natal" title="Colony of Natal">Colony of Natal</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Great Britain occupied Cape Town between 1795 and 1803 to prevent it from falling under the control of the <a href="/wiki/French_First_Republic" title="French First Republic">French First Republic</a>, which had invaded the <a href="/wiki/Low_Countries" title="Low Countries">Low Countries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Stapleton2_47-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stapleton2-47">[47]</a></sup> After briefly returning to Dutch rule under the <a href="/wiki/Batavian_Republic" title="Batavian Republic">Batavian Republic</a> in 1803, the Cape was occupied again by the British in 1806.<sup id="cite_ref-Keegan_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keegan-48">[48]</a></sup> Following the end of the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, it was formally ceded to Great Britain and became an integral part of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lloyd1_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lloyd1-49">[49]</a></sup> British emigration to South Africa began around 1818, subsequently culminating in the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/1820_Settlers" title="1820 Settlers">1820 Settlers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lloyd1_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lloyd1-49">[49]</a></sup> The new colonists were induced to settle for a variety of reasons, namely to increase the size of the European workforce and to bolster frontier regions against Xhosa incursions.<sup id="cite_ref-Lloyd1_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lloyd1-49">[49]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Bell_-_Zoeloe-aanval_op_%27n_Boerelaer_-_1838.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Charles_Bell_-_Zoeloe-aanval_op_%27n_Boerelaer_-_1838.jpg/220px-Charles_Bell_-_Zoeloe-aanval_op_%27n_Boerelaer_-_1838.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Charles_Bell_-_Zoeloe-aanval_op_%27n_Boerelaer_-_1838.jpg/330px-Charles_Bell_-_Zoeloe-aanval_op_%27n_Boerelaer_-_1838.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Charles_Bell_-_Zoeloe-aanval_op_%27n_Boerelaer_-_1838.jpg/440px-Charles_Bell_-_Zoeloe-aanval_op_%27n_Boerelaer_-_1838.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1376" data-file-height="1087"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Bell_-_Zoeloe-aanval_op_%27n_Boerelaer_-_1838.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Depiction of a <a href="/wiki/Weenen_massacre" title="Weenen massacre">Zulu attack</a> on a Boer camp in February 1838
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In the first two decades of the 19th century, the Zulu people grew in power and expanded their territory under their leader, <a href="/wiki/Shaka" title="Shaka">Shaka</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Shaka's warfare indirectly led to the <i lang="zu" title="Zulu-language text"><a href="/wiki/Mfecane" title="Mfecane">Mfecane</a></i> ('crushing'), in which 1,000,000 to 2,000,000 people were killed and the inland plateau was devastated and depopulated in the early 1820s.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> An offshoot of the Zulu, the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ndebele_people" title="Northern Ndebele people">Matabele</a> people created a larger empire that included large parts of the <a href="/wiki/Highveld" title="Highveld">highveld</a> under their king <a href="/wiki/Mzilikazi" title="Mzilikazi">Mzilikazi</a>. </p>
      <p>During the early 1800s, many Dutch settlers departed from the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Colony" title="Cape Colony">Cape Colony</a>, where they had been subjected to British control, in a series of migrant groups who came to be known as <i lang="nl" title="Dutch-language text"><a href="/wiki/Voortrekkers" class="mw-redirect" title="Voortrekkers">Voortrekkers</a></i>, meaning "pathfinders" or "pioneers". They migrated to the future <a href="/wiki/Natal_(province)" title="Natal (province)">Natal</a>, Free State, and <a href="/wiki/Transvaal_(province)" title="Transvaal (province)">Transvaal</a> regions. The Boers founded the <a href="/wiki/Boer_Republics" title="Boer Republics">Boer Republics</a>: the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Republic" title="South African Republic">South African Republic</a> (now Gauteng, Limpopo, Mpumalanga and <a href="/wiki/North_West_Province_(South_Africa)" class="mw-redirect" title="North West Province (South Africa)">North West</a> provinces), the <a href="/wiki/Natalia_Republic" title="Natalia Republic">Natalia Republic</a> (KwaZulu-Natal), and the <a href="/wiki/Orange_Free_State" title="Orange Free State">Orange Free State</a> (Free State). </p>
      <p>The discovery of diamonds in 1867 and gold in 1884 in the interior started the <a href="/wiki/Mineral_Revolution" title="Mineral Revolution">Mineral Revolution</a> and increased economic growth and immigration. This intensified British efforts to gain control over the indigenous peoples. The struggle to control these important economic resources was a factor in relations between Europeans and the indigenous population and also between the Boers and the British.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 16 May 1876, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Fran%C3%A7ois_Burgers" title="Thomas François Burgers">President Thomas François Burgers</a> of the South African Republic (<a href="/wiki/Transvaal_Colony" title="Transvaal Colony">Transvaal</a>) declared war against <a href="/wiki/Sekhukhune" title="Sekhukhune">Sekhukhune</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pedi_people" title="Pedi people">Pedi</a>. Sekhukhune managed to defeat the Transvaal army on 1 August 1876. Another attack by the <i>Lydenburg Volunteer Corps</i> was also repulsed. On 16 February 1877, the two parties signed a peace treaty at <a href="/wiki/Botshabelo,_Mpumalanga" title="Botshabelo, Mpumalanga">Botshabelo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The Boers' inability to subdue Sekhukhune and the Pedi led to the departure of Burgers in favour of <a href="/wiki/Paul_Kruger" title="Paul Kruger">Paul Kruger</a> and the British annexation of the South African Republic (<a href="/wiki/Transvaal_Colony" title="Transvaal Colony">Transvaal</a>) on 12 April 1877 by <a href="/wiki/Theophilus_Shepstone" title="Theophilus Shepstone">Theophilus Shepstone</a>, secretary for native affairs of <a href="/wiki/Colony_of_Natal" title="Colony of Natal">Natal</a>. In 1878 and 1879 three British attacks were successfully repelled until <a href="/wiki/Garnet_Wolseley,_1st_Viscount_Wolseley" title="Garnet Wolseley, 1st Viscount Wolseley">Garnet Wolseley</a> defeated Sekhukhune in November 1879 with an army of 2,000 British soldiers, Boers and 10,000 Swazis. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Zulu_War" title="Anglo-Zulu War">Anglo-Zulu War</a> was fought in 1879 between the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Zulu_Kingdom" title="Zulu Kingdom">Zulu Kingdom</a>. Following <a href="/wiki/Lord_Carnarvon" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Carnarvon">Lord Carnarvon</a>'s successful introduction of <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Confederation" title="Canadian Confederation">federation in Canada</a>, it was thought that similar political effort, coupled with military campaigns, might succeed with the African kingdoms, tribal areas and <a href="/wiki/Boer_republics" class="mw-redirect" title="Boer republics">Boer republics</a> in South Africa. In 1874, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Bartle_Frere" title="Henry Bartle Frere">Henry Bartle Frere</a> was sent to South Africa as the British <a href="/wiki/High_Commissioner_for_Southern_Africa" title="High Commissioner for Southern Africa">High Commissioner</a> to bring such plans into being. Among the obstacles were the presence of the independent states of the Boers, and the <a href="/wiki/Zulu_Kingdom" title="Zulu Kingdom">Kingdom of Zululand</a>'s army. The Zulu nation defeated the British at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Isandlwana" title="Battle of Isandlwana">Battle of Isandlwana</a>. Eventually, though, the war was lost, resulting in the termination of the Zulu nation's independence. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Boer_Wars">Boer Wars</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/First_Boer_War" title="First Boer War">First Boer War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Second Boer War</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Boers_1881.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Boers_1881.png/220px-Boers_1881.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="232" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Boers_1881.png/330px-Boers_1881.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c2/Boers_1881.png 2x" data-file-width="380" data-file-height="401"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Boers_1881.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The First Boer War was a rebellion of <a href="/wiki/Boer" class="mw-redirect" title="Boer">Boers</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Transvaal_Colony" title="Transvaal Colony">British rule in the Transvaal</a> that re-established their independence.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Boer_Republics" title="Boer Republics">Boer Republics</a> successfully resisted British encroachments during the <a href="/wiki/First_Boer_War" title="First Boer War">First Boer War</a> (1880–1881) using <a href="/wiki/Guerrilla_warfare" title="Guerrilla warfare">guerrilla warfare</a> tactics, which were well-suited to local conditions. The British returned with greater numbers, more experience, and new strategy in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Second Boer War</a> (1899–1902) but suffered heavy casualties through <a href="/wiki/Attrition_warfare" title="Attrition warfare">attrition</a>; nonetheless, they were ultimately successful. Over 27,000 Boer women and children died in the <a href="/wiki/British_concentration_camps" class="mw-redirect" title="British concentration camps">British concentration camps</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence">Independence</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_South_Africa_during_World_War_I" title="Military history of South Africa during World War I">Military history of South Africa during World War I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_South_Africa_during_World_War_II" title="Military history of South Africa during World War II">Military history of South Africa during World War II</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Within the country, anti-British policies among white South Africans focused on independence. During the Dutch and British colonial years, <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation" title="Racial segregation">racial segregation</a> was mostly informal, though some legislation was enacted to control the settlement and movement of indigenous people, including the <a href="/wiki/Native_Location_Act_of_1879" title="Native Location Act of 1879">Native Location Act of 1879</a> and the system of <a href="/wiki/Pass_laws" title="Pass laws">pass laws</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Eight years after the end of the Second Boer War and after four years of negotiation, an act of the British Parliament (<a href="/wiki/South_Africa_Act_1909" title="South Africa Act 1909">South Africa Act 1909</a>) granted nominal independence, while creating the <a href="/wiki/Union_of_South_Africa" title="Union of South Africa">Union of South Africa</a> on 31 May 1910. The Union was a <a href="/wiki/Dominion" title="Dominion">dominion</a> that included the former territories of the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Colony" title="Cape Colony">Cape</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transvaal_Colony" title="Transvaal Colony">Transvaal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colony_of_Natal" title="Colony of Natal">Natal</a> colonies, as well as the Orange Free State republic.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Natives%27_Land_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Natives' Land Act">Natives' Land Act</a> of 1913 severely restricted the ownership of land by blacks; at that stage they controlled only seven percent of the country. The amount of land reserved for <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">indigenous peoples</a> was later marginally increased.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1931, the union was fully sovereign from the United Kingdom with the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_Westminster_1931" title="Statute of Westminster 1931">Statute of Westminster</a>, which abolished the last powers of the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament of the United Kingdom</a> to legislate on the country. In 1934, the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Party" title="South African Party">South African Party</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Party_(South_Africa)" title="National Party (South Africa)">National Party</a> merged to form the <a href="/wiki/United_Party_(South_Africa)" title="United Party (South Africa)">United Party</a>, seeking reconciliation between Afrikaners and English-speaking whites. In 1939, the party split over the entry of the Union into <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> as an <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_South_Africa_during_World_War_II" title="Military history of South Africa during World War II">ally of the United Kingdom</a>, a move which the National Party followers strongly opposed. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beginning_of_apartheid">Beginning of apartheid</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">Apartheid</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:ApartheidSignEnglishAfrikaans.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/ApartheidSignEnglishAfrikaans.jpg/220px-ApartheidSignEnglishAfrikaans.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="201" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/ApartheidSignEnglishAfrikaans.jpg/330px-ApartheidSignEnglishAfrikaans.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/ApartheidSignEnglishAfrikaans.jpg/440px-ApartheidSignEnglishAfrikaans.jpg 2x" data-file-width="665" data-file-height="609"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:ApartheidSignEnglishAfrikaans.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>"For use by white persons"&nbsp;– apartheid sign in English and Afrikaans
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1948, the National Party was elected to power. It strengthened the racial segregation begun under Dutch and British colonial rule. Taking Canada's <a href="/wiki/Indian_Act" title="Indian Act">Indian Act</a> as a framework,<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalist</a> government classified all peoples into three races and developed rights and limitations for each. The white minority (less than 20%)<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> controlled the vastly larger black majority. The legally institutionalised segregation became known as <i><a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">apartheid</a></i>. While whites enjoyed the highest <a href="/wiki/Standard_of_living" title="Standard of living">standard of living</a> in all of Africa, comparable to <a href="/wiki/First_World" title="First World">First World</a> Western nations, the black majority remained disadvantaged by almost every standard, including income, education, housing, and life expectancy. The <a href="/wiki/Freedom_Charter" title="Freedom Charter">Freedom Charter</a>, adopted in 1955 by the <a href="/wiki/Congress_Alliance" title="Congress Alliance">Congress Alliance</a>, demanded a non-racial society and an end to discrimination. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Republic">Republic</span></h3> 
      <p>On 31 May 1961, the country became a republic following <a href="/wiki/South_African_republic_referendum,_1960" class="mw-redirect" title="South African republic referendum, 1960">a referendum</a> (only open to white voters) which narrowly passed;<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> the British-dominated <a href="/wiki/Natal_Province" class="mw-redirect" title="Natal Province">Natal</a> province largely voted against the proposal. <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Elizabeth II">Queen Elizabeth II</a> lost the title <a href="/wiki/Queen_of_South_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen of South Africa">Queen of South Africa</a>, and the last <a href="/wiki/Governor-General_of_the_Union_of_South_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Governor-General of the Union of South Africa">Governor-General</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Robberts_Swart" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Robberts Swart">Charles Robberts Swart</a>, became <a href="/wiki/State_President_of_South_Africa" title="State President of South Africa">State President</a>. As a concession to the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_system" title="Westminster system">Westminster system</a>, the appointment of the president remained an appointment by parliament, and virtually powerless until <a href="/wiki/P._W._Botha" title="P. W. Botha">P. W. Botha</a>'s <a href="/wiki/South_African_Constitution_of_1983" title="South African Constitution of 1983">Constitution Act of 1983</a>, which eliminated the office of <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_South_Africa" title="Prime Minister of South Africa">Prime Minister</a> and instated a near-unique "strong presidency" <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_system" title="Parliamentary system">responsible to parliament</a>. Pressured by other <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a> countries, South Africa withdrew from the organisation in 1961 and rejoined it only in 1994. </p>
      <p>Despite <a href="/wiki/Internal_resistance_to_apartheid" title="Internal resistance to apartheid">opposition</a> both within and outside the country, the government legislated for a continuation of apartheid. The security forces cracked down on internal dissent, and violence became widespread, with anti-apartheid organisations such as the <a href="/wiki/African_National_Congress" title="African National Congress">African National Congress</a> (ANC), the <a href="/wiki/Azanian_People%27s_Organisation" title="Azanian People's Organisation">Azanian People's Organisation</a> (AZAPO), and the <a href="/wiki/Pan_Africanist_Congress_of_Azania" title="Pan Africanist Congress of Azania">Pan-Africanist Congress</a> (PAC) carrying out guerrilla warfare<sup id="cite_ref-Biko1_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biko1-66">[66]</a></sup> and urban <a href="/wiki/Sabotage" title="Sabotage">sabotage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Switzer_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Switzer-67">[67]</a></sup> The three rival resistance movements also engaged in occasional inter-factional clashes as they jockeyed for domestic influence.<sup id="cite_ref-Mitchell_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell-68">[68]</a></sup> Apartheid became increasingly controversial, and several countries began to <a href="/wiki/Boycott" title="Boycott">boycott</a> business with the South African government because of its racial policies. These measures were later extended to <a href="/wiki/International_sanctions" title="International sanctions">international sanctions</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Disinvestment_from_South_Africa" title="Disinvestment from South Africa">divestment of holdings</a> by foreign investors.<sup id="cite_ref-Bridgland_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bridgland-69">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Landgren_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Landgren-70">[70]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the late 1970s, South Africa <a href="/wiki/South_Africa_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction" title="South Africa and weapons of mass destruction">initiated a programme of nuclear weapons development</a>. In the following decade, it produced six deliverable nuclear weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_apartheid">End of apartheid</span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_South_Africa_(1994%E2%80%93present)" title="History of South Africa (1994–present)">History of South Africa (1994–present)</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Frederik_de_Klerk_with_Nelson_Mandela_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_1992.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Frederik_de_Klerk_with_Nelson_Mandela_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_1992.jpg/280px-Frederik_de_Klerk_with_Nelson_Mandela_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_1992.jpg" decoding="async" width="280" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Frederik_de_Klerk_with_Nelson_Mandela_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_1992.jpg/420px-Frederik_de_Klerk_with_Nelson_Mandela_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_1992.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Frederik_de_Klerk_with_Nelson_Mandela_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_1992.jpg/560px-Frederik_de_Klerk_with_Nelson_Mandela_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_1992.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="625"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Frederik_de_Klerk_with_Nelson_Mandela_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_1992.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/F._W._de_Klerk" title="F. W. de Klerk">FW de Klerk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela" title="Nelson Mandela">Nelson Mandela</a> shake hands in January 1992
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Mahlabatini_Declaration" class="mw-redirect" title="Mahlabatini Declaration">Mahlabatini Declaration</a> of Faith, signed by <a href="/wiki/Mangosuthu_Buthelezi" title="Mangosuthu Buthelezi">Mangosuthu Buthelezi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harry_Schwarz" title="Harry Schwarz">Harry Schwarz</a> in 1974, enshrined the principles of <a href="/wiki/Peaceful_transition_of_power" title="Peaceful transition of power">peaceful transition of power</a> and equality for all, the first of such agreements by black and white political leaders in South Africa. Ultimately, <a href="/wiki/F._W._de_Klerk" title="F. W. de Klerk">FW de Klerk</a> opened bilateral discussions with <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela" title="Nelson Mandela">Nelson Mandela</a> in 1993 for a transition of policies and government. </p>
      <p>In 1990, the National Party government took the first step towards dismantling discrimination when it lifted the ban on the ANC and other political organisations. It released Nelson Mandela from prison after 27 years' serving a sentence for sabotage. A <a href="/wiki/Negotiations_to_end_apartheid_in_South_Africa" title="Negotiations to end apartheid in South Africa">negotiation process</a> followed. With approval from the white electorate in a <a href="/wiki/South_African_apartheid_referendum,_1992" class="mw-redirect" title="South African apartheid referendum, 1992">1992 referendum</a>, the government continued negotiations to end apartheid. South Africa also destroyed its nuclear arsenal and acceded to the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Non-Proliferation_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear Non-Proliferation Treaty">Nuclear Non-Proliferation Treaty</a>. South Africa held its first universal <a href="/wiki/South_African_general_election,_1994" class="mw-redirect" title="South African general election, 1994">elections in 1994</a>, which the ANC won by an overwhelming majority. It has been in power ever since. The country rejoined the Commonwealth of Nations and became a member of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_African_Development_Community" title="Southern African Development Community">Southern African Development Community</a> (SADC). </p>
      <p>In post-apartheid South Africa, unemployment remained high. While many blacks have risen to middle or upper classes, the overall unemployment rate of black people worsened between 1994 and 2003 by official metrics, but declined significantly using expanded definitions.<sup id="cite_ref-sach3_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sach3-73">[73]</a></sup> Poverty among whites, which was previously rare, increased.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> In addition, the current government has struggled to achieve the monetary and fiscal discipline to ensure both redistribution of wealth and economic growth. The United Nations (UN) <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a> (HDI) of South Africa fell from 1995 to 2005, while it was steadily rising until the mid-1990s,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> before recovering its 1995 peak in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> This is in large part attributable to the <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS_in_South_Africa" title="HIV/AIDS in South Africa">South African HIV/AIDS pandemic</a> which saw South African life expectancy fall from a high point of 62.25 years in 1992 to a low of 52.57 in 2005,<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> and the failure of the government to take steps to address the pandemic in its early years.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In May 2008, riots left over 60 people dead.<sup id="cite_ref-Broke-on-Broke_Violence_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Broke-on-Broke_Violence-79">[79]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Centre_on_Housing_Rights_and_Evictions" title="Centre on Housing Rights and Evictions">Centre on Housing Rights and Evictions</a> estimated that over 100,000 people were driven from their homes.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> The targets were mainly <a href="/wiki/Immigration_to_South_Africa" title="Immigration to South Africa">legal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Illegal_immigration_to_South_Africa" title="Illegal immigration to South Africa">illegal migrants</a>, and refugees seeking asylum, but a third of the victims were South African citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-Broke-on-Broke_Violence_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Broke-on-Broke_Violence-79">[79]</a></sup> In a 2006 survey, the South African Migration Project concluded that South Africans are more opposed to immigration than any other national group.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_High_Commissioner_for_Refugees" title="United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees">UN High Commissioner for Refugees</a> in 2008 reported over 200,000 refugees applied for asylum in South Africa, almost four times as many as the year before.<sup id="cite_ref-unhcr.org_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unhcr.org-82">[82]</a></sup> These people were mainly from <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>, though many also come from <a href="/wiki/Burundi" title="Burundi">Burundi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-unhcr.org_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unhcr.org-82">[82]</a></sup> Competition over jobs, business opportunities, public services and housing has led to tension between refugees and host communities.<sup id="cite_ref-unhcr.org_82-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unhcr.org-82">[82]</a></sup> While <a href="/wiki/Xenophobia_in_South_Africa" title="Xenophobia in South Africa">xenophobia in South Africa</a> is still a problem, recent violence has not been as widespread as initially feared.<sup id="cite_ref-unhcr.org_82-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unhcr.org-82">[82]</a></sup> Nevertheless, as South Africa continues to grapple with racial issues, one of the proposed solutions has been to pass legislation, such as the pending <a href="/wiki/The_Prevention_and_Combating_of_Hate_Crimes_and_Hate_Speech_Bill" title="The Prevention and Combating of Hate Crimes and Hate Speech Bill">Hate Crimes and Hate Speech Bill</a>, to uphold South Africa's ban on racism and commitment to equality.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_South_Africa" title="Geography of South Africa">Geography of South Africa</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Southern_African_Central_Plateau.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Southern_African_Central_Plateau.jpg/280px-Southern_African_Central_Plateau.jpg" decoding="async" width="280" height="190" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Southern_African_Central_Plateau.jpg/420px-Southern_African_Central_Plateau.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Southern_African_Central_Plateau.jpg/560px-Southern_African_Central_Plateau.jpg 2x" data-file-width="9960" data-file-height="6756"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Southern_African_Central_Plateau.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A map of South Africa showing the main topographic features: the Central Plateau edged by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Escarpment,_Southern_Africa" title="Great Escarpment, Southern Africa">Great Escarpment</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Fold_Belt" title="Cape Fold Belt">Cape Fold Belt</a> in the south-west corner of the country
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Regions_of_South_Africa_1.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Regions_of_South_Africa_1.png/280px-Regions_of_South_Africa_1.png" decoding="async" width="280" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Regions_of_South_Africa_1.png/420px-Regions_of_South_Africa_1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Regions_of_South_Africa_1.png/560px-Regions_of_South_Africa_1.png 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1394"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Regions_of_South_Africa_1.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Important geographical regions in South Africa. The thick line traces the course of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Escarpment,_Southern_Africa" title="Great Escarpment, Southern Africa">Great Escarpment</a> which edges the central plateau. The eastern portion of this line, coloured red, is the <a href="/wiki/Drakensberg" title="Drakensberg">Drakensberg</a>. The Escarpment rises to its highest point, at over 3,000&nbsp;m (9,800&nbsp;ft), where the Drakensberg forms the border between <a href="/wiki/KwaZulu-Natal" title="KwaZulu-Natal">KwaZulu-Natal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lesotho" title="Lesotho">Lesotho</a>. None of the regions indicated on the map has a well-defined border, except where the Escarpment or a mountain range forms a clear dividing line between regions. Some of the better-known regions are coloured in; their names simply indicate the others.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>South Africa is located at the southernmost region of Africa, with a long coastline that stretches more than 2,500&nbsp;km (1,553&nbsp;mi) and along two oceans (the South Atlantic and the Indian). At 1,219,912&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup> (471,011&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi),<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> South Africa is the 24th-largest country in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> It is about the same size as <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, twice the size of France, three times as big as Japan, four times the size of Italy and five times the size of the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Mafadi" title="Mafadi">Mafadi</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Drakensberg" title="Drakensberg">Drakensberg</a> at 3,450&nbsp;m (11,320&nbsp;ft) is the highest peak in South Africa. Excluding the <a href="/wiki/Prince_Edward_Islands" title="Prince Edward Islands">Prince Edward Islands</a>, the country lies between latitudes <a href="/wiki/22nd_parallel_south" title="22nd parallel south">22°</a> and <a href="/wiki/35th_parallel_south" title="35th parallel south">35°S</a>, and longitudes <a href="/wiki/16th_meridian_east" title="16th meridian east">16°</a> and <a href="/wiki/33rd_meridian_east" title="33rd meridian east">33°E</a>. </p>
      <p>The interior of South Africa consists of a vast, in most places almost flat, plateau with an altitude of between 1,000&nbsp;m (3,300&nbsp;ft) and 2,100&nbsp;m (6,900&nbsp;ft), highest in the east and sloping gently downwards towards the west and north, and slightly less noticeably so to the south and south-west.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> This plateau is surrounded by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Escarpment,_Southern_Africa" title="Great Escarpment, Southern Africa">Great Escarpment</a><sup id="cite_ref-Altas_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Altas-89">[89]</a></sup> whose eastern, and highest, stretch is known as the Drakensberg.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The south and south-western parts of the plateau (at approximately 1,100–1,800<span class="nowrap">&nbsp;</span>m above sea level), and the adjoining plain below (at approximately 700–800<span class="nowrap">&nbsp;</span>m above sea level&nbsp;–&nbsp;see map on the right) is known as the <a href="/wiki/Karoo" title="Karoo">Great Karoo</a>, which consists of sparsely populated <a href="/wiki/Scrubland" class="mw-redirect" title="Scrubland">scrubland</a>. To the north, the Great Karoo fades into the even drier and more arid Bushmanland, which eventually becomes the <a href="/wiki/Kalahari" class="mw-redirect" title="Kalahari">Kalahari</a> desert in the very north-west of the country. The mid-eastern, and highest part of the plateau is known as the <a href="/wiki/Highveld" title="Highveld">Highveld</a>. This relatively well-watered area is home to a great proportion of the country's commercial farmlands and contains its largest conurbation (Gauteng). To the north of Highveld, from about the 25°<span class="nowrap">&nbsp;</span>30'<span class="nowrap">&nbsp;</span>S line of latitude, the plateau slopes downwards into the <a href="/wiki/Bushveld" title="Bushveld">Bushveld</a>, which ultimately gives way to the Limpopo lowlands or <a href="/wiki/Veld#Highveld_and_Lowveld" title="Veld">Lowveld</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Altas_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Altas-89">[89]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The coastal belt, below the Great Escarpment, moving clockwise from the northeast, consists of the Limpopo Lowveld, which merges into the Mpumalanga Lowveld, below the Mpumalanga Drakensberg (the eastern portion of the Great Escarpment).<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> This is hotter, drier and less intensely cultivated than the Highveld above the escarpment.<sup id="cite_ref-Altas_89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Altas-89">[89]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Kruger_National_Park" title="Kruger National Park">Kruger National Park</a>, located in the provinces of Limpopo and Mpumalanga in northeastern South Africa, occupies a large portion of the Lowveld covering 19,633 square kilometres (7,580 sq mi.)<sup id="cite_ref-Kruger_National_Park_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kruger_National_Park-92">[92]</a></sup> South of the Lowveld the annual rainfall increases as one enters KwaZulu-Natal Province, which, especially near the coast, is <a href="/wiki/Subtropics" title="Subtropics">subtropically</a> hot and humid. The KwaZulu-Natal–Lesotho international border is formed by the highest portion of the Great Escarpment, or Drakensberg, which reaches an altitude of over 3,000&nbsp;m (9,800&nbsp;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> The climate at the foot of this part of the Drakensberg is temperate. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:South_Africa_-_Drakensberg_(16261357780).jpg" class="image"><img alt="Image depicting the Drakensberg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/South_Africa_-_Drakensberg_%2816261357780%29.jpg/220px-South_Africa_-_Drakensberg_%2816261357780%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/South_Africa_-_Drakensberg_%2816261357780%29.jpg/330px-South_Africa_-_Drakensberg_%2816261357780%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/South_Africa_-_Drakensberg_%2816261357780%29.jpg/440px-South_Africa_-_Drakensberg_%2816261357780%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5313" data-file-height="2989"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:South_Africa_-_Drakensberg_(16261357780).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Drakensberg" title="Drakensberg">Drakensberg</a>, the eastern and highest portion of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Escarpment,_Southern_Africa" title="Great Escarpment, Southern Africa">Great Escarpment</a> which surrounds the east, south and western borders of the central plateau of Southern Africa
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The coastal belt below the south and south-western stretches of the Great Escarpment contains several ranges of <a href="/wiki/Cape_Fold_Belt" title="Cape Fold Belt">Cape Fold Mountains</a> which run parallel to the coast, separating the Great Escarpment from the ocean.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-geological_map_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-geological_map-95">[95]</a></sup> (These parallel ranges of fold mountains are shown on the map, above left. Note the course of the Great Escarpment to the north of these mountain ranges.) The land (at approximately 400–500<span class="nowrap">&nbsp;</span>m above sea level) between two of these ranges of fold mountains in the south (i.e. between the <a href="/wiki/Outeniqua_Mountains" title="Outeniqua Mountains">Outeniqua</a> and <a href="/wiki/Langeberg" title="Langeberg">Langeberg</a> ranges to the south and the <a href="/wiki/Swartberg" title="Swartberg">Swartberg</a> range to the north) is known as the <a href="/wiki/Karoo" title="Karoo">Little Karoo</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Altas_89-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Altas-89">[89]</a></sup> which consists of semi-desert scrubland similar to that of the Great Karoo, except that its northern strip along the foothills of the Swartberg Mountains, has a somewhat higher rainfall and is, therefore, more cultivated than the Great Karoo. The Little Karoo is historically, and still, famous for its ostrich farming around the town of <a href="/wiki/Oudtshoorn" title="Oudtshoorn">Oudtshoorn</a>. The lowland area (700–800<span class="nowrap">&nbsp;</span>m above sea level) to the north of the Swartberg mountain range up to the Great Escarpment is the lowland part of the Great Karoo (see map at top right), which is climatically and botanically almost indistinguishable from the Karoo above the Great Escarpment. The narrow coastal strip between the most seaward Cape Fold Mountain range (i.e., the Langeberg–Outeniqua mountains) and the ocean has a moderately high year-round rainfall, especially in the <a href="/wiki/George,_Western_Cape" title="George, Western Cape">George</a>-<a href="/wiki/Knysna" title="Knysna">Knysna</a>-<a href="/wiki/Plettenberg_Bay" title="Plettenberg Bay">Plettenberg Bay</a> region, which is known as the <a href="/wiki/Garden_Route" title="Garden Route">Garden Route</a>. It is famous for the most extensive areas of indigenous forests in South Africa (a generally forest-poor country). </p>
      <p>In the south-west corner of the country, the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Peninsula" title="Cape Peninsula">Cape Peninsula</a> forms the southernmost tip of the coastal strip which borders the Atlantic Ocean and ultimately terminates at the country's border with Namibia at the <a href="/wiki/Orange_River" title="Orange River">Orange River</a>. The Cape Peninsula has a <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_climate" title="Mediterranean climate">Mediterranean climate</a>, making it and its immediate surrounds the only portion of <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Africa south of the Sahara</a> which receives most of its rainfall in winter.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Altas1_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Altas1-97">[97]</a></sup> The greater <a href="/wiki/City_of_Cape_Town" title="City of Cape Town">Cape Town metropolitan area</a> is situated on the Cape Peninsula and is home to 3.7<span class="nowrap">&nbsp;</span>million people according to the 2011 population census. It is the country's legislative capital. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Namaqualand,_Goegap_0035.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Namaqualand%2C_Goegap_0035.jpg/220px-Namaqualand%2C_Goegap_0035.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Namaqualand%2C_Goegap_0035.jpg/330px-Namaqualand%2C_Goegap_0035.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Namaqualand%2C_Goegap_0035.jpg/440px-Namaqualand%2C_Goegap_0035.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Namaqualand,_Goegap_0035.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Spring flowers in <a href="/wiki/Namaqualand" title="Namaqualand">Namaqualand</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The coastal belt to the north of the Cape Peninsula is bounded on the west by the Atlantic Ocean and the first row of north–south running Cape Fold Mountains to the east. The Cape Fold Mountains peter out at about the 32°<span class="nowrap">&nbsp;</span>S line of latitude,<sup id="cite_ref-geological_map_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-geological_map-95">[95]</a></sup> after which the Great Escarpment itself bounds the coastal plain. The most southerly portion of this coastal belt is known as the <a href="/wiki/Swartland" title="Swartland">Swartland</a> and Malmesbury Plain, which is an important wheat growing region, relying on winter rains. The region further north is known as <a href="/wiki/Namaqualand" title="Namaqualand">Namaqualand</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> which becomes more and more arid as one approaches the <a href="/wiki/Orange_River" title="Orange River">Orange River</a>. The little rain that falls tends to fall in winter,<sup id="cite_ref-Altas1_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Altas1-97">[97]</a></sup> which results in one of the world's most spectacular displays of flowers carpeting huge stretches of <a href="/wiki/Veld" title="Veld">veld</a> in spring (August–September). </p>
